The proclamation is read out in X's village, affixed to his house, and published in a local newspaper. X fails to appear. The Court declares X a proclaimed offender.

BNSS SECTION 85 - ATTACHMENT OF PROPERTY OF PERSON ABSCONDING
Core Content:
The Court issuing a proclamation under section 84 may, for reasons recorded in writing, order attachment of any property (movable or immovable) belonging to the proclaimed person. If the Court is satisfied that the person is about to dispose of or remove property, it may order attachment simultaneously with the proclamation.

Procedure: Attachment of Property

Court issues proclamation under section 84

Court may, for reasons recorded in writing, at any time after issue of proclamation, order attachment of any property (movable or immovable) belonging to proclaimed person

Simultaneous attachment: If at time of issuing proclamation Court is satisfied by affidavit that person is:

(a) about to dispose of whole or part of property, OR

(b) about to remove whole or part of property from local jurisdiction of Court
→ Court may order attachment simultaneously with issue of proclamation

Attachment of movable property (debt or other movable):

(a) by seizure, OR

(b) by appointment of receiver, OR

(c) by order prohibiting delivery to proclaimed person, OR

(d) by all or any two of these methods

Attachment of immovable property (non-revenue paying land):

(a) by taking possession, OR

(b) by appointment of receiver, OR

(c) by order prohibiting payment of rent/delivery to proclaimed person, OR

(d) by all or any two methods

For land paying revenue to State: attachment through Collector of the district

For livestock or perishable property: Court may order immediate sale

Illustration:
X absconds after a warrant is issued for his arrest for a serious crime. The Court issues a proclamation and simultaneously orders attachment of X's house and bank account. The Court reasons that X might sell the property to avoid forfeiture. The bank account is frozen by an order prohibiting delivery to X.

BNSS SECTION 86 - IDENTIFICATION AND ATTACHMENT OF PROPERTY OF PROCLAIMED PERSON
Core Content:
The Court may, on the written request from a police officer not below the rank of Superintendent of Police or Commissioner of Police, initiate the process of requesting assistance from a Court or authority in a contracting State for identification, attachment, and forfeiture of property belonging to a proclaimed person in accordance with Chapter VIII procedure.

Procedure: International Asset Tracing

Police officer not below rank of Superintendent of Police or Commissioner of Police makes written request to Court

Court may initiate process of requesting assistance from:

A Court or authority in a contracting State (country outside India with reciprocal arrangements)

Purpose: identification, attachment, and forfeiture of property belonging to a proclaimed person

Procedure to be followed as provided in Chapter VIII (Reciprocal Arrangements for Assistance)

Illustration:
X is declared a proclaimed offender and has fled to Dubai. The Superintendent of Police makes a written request to the Court to seek assistance from UAE authorities. The Court initiates process under Chapter VIII to identify and attach any property X owns in Dubai.

BNSS SECTION 87 - CLAIMS AND OBJECTIONS TO ATTACHMENT
Core Content:
Any person other than the proclaimed person may prefer a claim or objection to attachment of property within six months from the date of attachment, on the ground that he has an interest not liable to attachment. The claim shall be inquired into and may be allowed or disallowed. If disallowed, the person may institute a suit within one year.

Procedure: Third Party Claims

Any person other than the proclaimed person may prefer claim or objection to attachment of property

Time limit: within 6 months from date of attachment

Ground: claimant/objector has an interest in such property and that interest is not liable to attachment under section 85

Claim or objection shall be inquired into and may be allowed or disallowed in whole or in part

If claimant/objector dies, legal representative may continue

Claim may be preferred in:

(i) Court which issued attachment order, OR

(ii) If property attached under endorsement under section 85(2), in Court of Chief Judicial Magistrate of district where attachment is made

If claim disallowed, person may institute a suit to establish right within 1 year from date of order

Illustration:
X's property is attached after X absconds. Y claims that the property actually belongs to Y and was only in X's name as a trustee. Y files a claim within 6 months. The Court inquires and disallows Y's claim. Y may file a civil suit within 1 year to prove ownership.

BNSS SECTION 88 - RELEASE, SALE AND RESTORATION OF ATTACHED PROPERTY
Core Content:
If the proclaimed person appears within the specified time, the Court shall release the property. If he does not appear, the property shall be at the disposal of the State Government but shall not be sold until after six months. If within two years the person proves he did not abscond to avoid warrant, the property or sale proceeds shall be delivered to him.

Procedure: Disposal of Attached Property

If proclaimed person appears within time specified in proclamation → Court shall release property from attachment

If proclaimed person does not appear within time specified:

Property shall be at disposal of State Government

BUT shall not be sold until expiration of 6 months from date of attachment

And until any claim under section 87 is disposed of

Exception: property subject to speedy and natural decay OR Court considers sale would benefit owner → Court may sell whenever it thinks fit

Restoration within 2 years: If within 2 years from date of attachment, person appears voluntarily or is apprehended and brought before Court, AND proves to Court's satisfaction that:

(i) He did not abscond or conceal himself for purpose of avoiding execution of warrant, AND

(ii) He had not such notice of the proclamation as to enable him to attend within specified time
→ Then property (or net proceeds of sale, or residue) shall be delivered to him after satisfying all costs

Illustration:
X's property is attached after he absconds. X does not appear within the proclamation period. The property is at State Government's disposal but cannot be sold for 6 months. After 18 months, X returns voluntarily and proves he was in a coma in a hospital and had no knowledge of the proclamation. The Court orders the property returned to X.

BNSS SECTION 89 - APPEAL FROM ORDER REJECTING APPLICATION FOR RESTORATION OF ATTACHED PROPERTY
Core Content:
Any person referred to in section 88(3) who is aggrieved by any refusal to deliver property or the proceeds of sale thereof may appeal to the Court to which appeals ordinarily lie from the sentences of the first-mentioned Court.

Procedure: Appeal Against Refusal

Person covered under section 88(3) (who proved he did not abscond to avoid warrant)

Aggrieved by refusal to deliver property or sale proceeds

May appeal to the Court to which appeals ordinarily lie from sentences of the first Court

Illustration:
The Court refused to restore X's property even after X proved he was in a coma and didn't abscond. X appeals to the higher Court (the Court that normally hears appeals from this Court's sentences). The Appellate Court reviews the decision.

BNSS SECTION 90 - ISSUE OF WARRANT IN LIEU OF, OR IN ADDITION TO, SUMMONS
Core Content:
A Court may, after recording its reasons in writing, issue a warrant for arrest instead of or in addition to a summons if it sees reason to believe that the person has absconded or will not obey the summons.

Procedure: Warrant Instead of Summons

Court is empowered to issue summons for appearance of any person

Court may, after recording reasons in writing, issue a warrant for his arrest:

(a) if before issue of summons, Court sees reason to believe he has absconded or will not obey summons, OR

(b) after issue of summons but before time fixed for appearance, Court sees reason to believe he has absconded or will not obey summons

Illustration:
The Court wants X to appear. The Court has information that X is planning to flee the country. The Court records this reason in writing and issues an arrest warrant directly, without issuing a summons first.

BNSS SECTION 91 - POWER TO TAKE BOND OR BAIL BOND FOR APPEARANCE
Core Content:
When any person for whose appearance or arrest the Court is empowered to issue a summons or warrant is present in such Court, the presiding officer may require such person to execute a bond or bail bond for his appearance in such Court or any other Court to which the case may be transferred for trial.

Procedure: Bond from Person Present in Court

Person is present in Court

Court could have issued summons or warrant for his appearance/arrest

Presiding officer may require such person to execute a bond or bail bond for his appearance in:

(i) Such Court, OR

(ii) Any other Court to which the case may be transferred for trial

Illustration:
X appears in Court voluntarily without being summoned. The Court, instead of issuing a summons, requires X to execute a bond promising to appear on the next hearing date. X signs the bond and is allowed to leave.

BNSS SECTION 92 - ARREST ON BREACH OF BOND OR BAIL BOND FOR APPEARANCE
Core Content:
When any person who is bound by any bond or bail bond taken under this Sanhita to appear before a Court does not appear, the officer presiding in such Court may issue a warrant directing that such person be arrested and produced before him.

Procedure: Arrest for Bond Breach

Person is bound by bond or bail bond to appear before a Court

Person does not appear as required

Presiding officer may issue a warrant for arrest

Warrant directs that the person be arrested and produced before him

Illustration:
X executed a bond to appear in Court on January 15. X fails to appear. The presiding officer issues an arrest warrant. Police arrest X and produce him before the Court. The Court may also forfeit the bond amount.

BNSS SECTION 93 - PROVISIONS OF THIS CHAPTER GENERALLY APPLICABLE TO SUMMONS AND WARRANTS OF ARREST
Core Content:
The provisions contained in this Chapter relating to summons and warrant, and their issue, service and execution, shall, so far as may be, apply to every summons and every warrant of arrest issued under this Sanhita.

Procedure: Universal Application

All provisions of Chapter VI (sections 63-93) relating to summons and warrants

Including their issue, service, and execution

Shall apply to every summons and every warrant of arrest issued under this Sanhita

"So far as may be" - applies as applicable to the specific situation

Illustration:
The rules for serving summons (section 64) and the rules for executing warrants (section 76) apply to all summons and warrants issued under BNSS, whether issued by a Magistrate, Sessions Court, or any other Criminal Court under BNSS.

BNSS SECTION 94 - SUMMONS TO PRODUCE DOCUMENT OR OTHER THING
Core Content:
Whenever any Court or any officer in charge of a police station considers that the production of any document, electronic communication, including communication devices which may contain digital evidence, or other thing is necessary for any investigation, inquiry, trial or other proceeding, such Court may issue a summons or such officer may issue a written order (physical or electronic form) requiring the person in whose possession it is to produce it at the stated time and place.

Procedure: Summons/Order to Produce

Court or officer in charge of police station considers production of document, electronic communication, communication device (likely containing digital evidence), or other thing is necessary or desirable for any investigation, inquiry, trial, or proceeding

Court may issue a summons

Police officer may issue a written order (physical or electronic form)

Require the person in whose possession or power such document/thing is believed to be to:

(i) Attend and produce it, OR

(ii) Produce it at the time and place stated

Person required merely to produce (not attend) shall be deemed to have complied if he causes such document/thing to be produced instead of attending personally

Does not affect sections 129 and 130 of Bharatiya Sakshya Adhiniyam, 2023 or Bankers' Books Evidence Act, 1891

Does not apply to letter, postcard, or other document or parcel or thing in custody of postal authority

Illustration:
During investigation of a cyber crime, the investigating officer believes X's laptop contains evidence. The officer issues a written order (by email) to X requiring him to produce the laptop at the police station at 10 AM tomorrow. X sends the laptop through his lawyer instead of coming personally - this is valid compliance.

BNSS SECTION 95 - PROCEDURE AS TO LETTERS
Core Content:
If any document, parcel or thing in custody of a postal authority is wanted for any investigation, inquiry, trial or proceeding, the District Magistrate, Chief Judicial Magistrate, Court of Session, or High Court may require the postal authority to deliver it to such person as directed. Any other Magistrate or Commissioner of Police or District Superintendent of Police may require the postal authority to search for and detain such item pending order of the higher authority.

Procedure: Obtaining Postal Items

Document, parcel, or thing is in custody of a postal authority

Wanted for purpose of any investigation, inquiry, trial, or proceeding under BNSS

Higher authorities (District Magistrate, Chief Judicial Magistrate, Court of Session, High Court) may require postal authority to deliver to such person as directed

Lower authorities (any other Magistrate (Executive or Judicial), Commissioner of Police, District Superintendent of Police) may require postal authority to:

(i) Cause search to be made for such document/parcel/thing

(ii) Detain it

(iii) Pending order of District Magistrate, Chief Judicial Magistrate, or Court

Illustration:
During investigation of a fraud case, the police believe that X has sent incriminating documents through registered post. The District Magistrate issues an order to the Post Master General to detain the parcel and deliver it to the investigating officer. The postal authority complies.

BNSS SECTION 96 - WHEN SEARCH-WARRANT MAY BE ISSUED
Core Content:
A Court may issue a search-warrant if: (a) a person summoned to produce a document/thing will not produce it, or (b) such document/thing is not known to be in anyone's possession, or (c) a general search or inspection would serve the purposes of inquiry/trial. Only District Magistrate or Chief Judicial Magistrate can grant warrant to search postal authority custody.

Procedure: Issuing Search-Warrant

Court may issue search-warrant when:

(a) Person to whom summons under section 94 or requisition under section 95(1) has been or might be addressed will not or would not produce the document/thing as required, OR

(b) Such document/thing is not known to the Court to be in possession of any person, OR

(c) Court considers that purposes of any inquiry, trial or proceeding will be served by a general search or inspection

Person to whom warrant is directed may search or inspect in accordance with the warrant

Court may specify particular place or part thereof to which search shall extend

Only District Magistrate or Chief Judicial Magistrate may grant warrant to search for document, parcel, or other thing in custody of postal authority

Illustration:
Court issued summons to X to produce a contract document. X refuses to produce it. The Court issues a search-warrant to the police authorising them to search X's office for the document. The police break open X's locker, find the document, and seize it.

BNSS SECTION 97 - SEARCH OF PLACE SUSPECTED TO CONTAIN STOLEN PROPERTY, FORGED DOCUMENTS, ETC.
Core Content:
If a District Magistrate, Sub-divisional Magistrate, or Magistrate of first class has reason to believe that any place is used for deposit/sale of stolen property or objectionable articles (counterfeit coin, forged documents, false seals, obscene objects, etc.), he may by warrant authorise a police officer above the rank of constable to enter, search, take possession, and arrest persons found there.

Procedure: Search for Stolen Property/Forged Items

Magistrate (District, Sub-divisional, or First Class) upon information and after inquiry has reason to believe:

Any place is used for deposit or sale of stolen property, OR

Any place is used for deposit, sale or production of any objectionable article, OR

Any objectionable article is deposited in any place

Magistrate may by warrant authorise police officer above rank of constable to:

(a) Enter with such assistance as required

(b) Search in manner specified

(c) Take possession of property/article reasonably suspected to be stolen or objectionable

(d) Convey such property/article before Magistrate or guard on spot or dispose in safe place

(e) Take into custody and carry before Magistrate every person found who appears privy to such deposit/sale/production knowing or suspecting it to be stolen/objectionable

Objectionable articles include:

(a) Counterfeit coin

(b) Pieces of metal made in contravention of Coinage Act, 2011 or brought into India violating Customs Act, 1962 notification

(c) Counterfeit currency note, counterfeit stamps

(d) Forged documents

(e) False seals

(f) Obscene objects under BNS section 294

(g) Instruments or materials used for production of any of the above

Illustration:
A District Magistrate receives information that a house is being used to manufacture counterfeit currency notes. After inquiry, the Magistrate issues a warrant to a police inspector authorising entry, search, seizure of counterfeit notes and printing equipment, and arrest of persons found inside. The inspector executes the warrant.

BNSS SECTION 98 - POWER TO DECLARE CERTAIN PUBLICATIONS FORFEITED AND TO ISSUE SEARCH-WARRANTS FOR SAME
Core Content:
If any newspaper, book, or document appears to the State Government to contain matter punishable under BNS sections 152, 196, 197, 294, 295, or 299, the State Government may by notification declare every copy forfeited to Government. Any police officer may seize the same, and any Magistrate may authorise a police officer not below sub-inspector to search premises.

Procedure: Forfeiture of Publications

State Government has power

Newspaper, book, or document (wherever printed) appears to contain matter whose publication is punishable under:

Section 152 (endangering sovereignty, unity, integrity of India)

Section 196 (promoting enmity between groups)

Section 197 (imputations prejudicial to national integration)

Section 294 (sale of obscene books, etc.)

Section 295 (sale of obscene objects to child)

Section 299 (deliberate acts outraging religious feelings)

State Government may by notification (stating grounds of opinion) declare:

Every copy of the issue of the newspaper containing such matter

Every copy of such book or other document
→ Forfeited to Government

Any police officer may seize the same wherever found in India

Any Magistrate may by warrant authorise police officer not below sub-inspector to enter upon and search any premises where any copy may be or may be reasonably suspected to be

"Newspaper" and "book" have same meanings as in Press and Registration of Books Act, 1867

"Document" includes painting, drawing, photograph, or other visible representation

No order or action under this section shall be called in question in any Court except under section 99

Illustration:
A newspaper publishes an article that the State Government believes promotes enmity between religious groups under BNS section 196. The State Government issues a notification declaring all copies of that newspaper issue forfeited. Police officers seize copies from newsstands. A Magistrate issues a warrant to search the newspaper's office for remaining copies.

BNSS SECTION 99 - APPLICATION TO HIGH COURT TO SET ASIDE DECLARATION OF FORFEITURE
Core Content:
Any person having an interest in any newspaper, book, or document forfeited under section 98 may, within two months from publication of the declaration in the Official Gazette, apply to the High Court to set aside the declaration on the ground that it did not contain such matter. The application shall be heard by a Special Bench of three Judges (or all Judges if less than three).

Procedure: Challenging Forfeiture

Any person having interest in forfeited newspaper, book, or document may apply to High Court

Time limit: within 2 months from date of publication of declaration in Official Gazette

Ground: the issue/book/document did not contain any such matter as referred to in section 98(1)

Application shall be heard by Special Bench of High Court:

If High Court has 3 or more Judges: Special Bench of 3 Judges

If High Court has less than 3 Judges: Special Bench of all Judges of that High Court

On hearing, any copy of such newspaper may be given in evidence to prove nature or tendency of the words/signs/representations

High Court shall set aside declaration if not satisfied that the issue/book/document contained such matter

If difference of opinion among Judges, decision follows majority opinion

Illustration:
The State Government declares a book forfeited under section 98. The publisher, X, files an application in the High Court within 2 months arguing the book does not contain any matter punishable under the specified BNS sections. The High Court constitutes a Special Bench of 3 Judges. After hearing, the Bench sets aside the forfeiture declaration.

BNSS SECTION 100 - SEARCH FOR PERSONS WRONGFULLY CONFINED
Core Content:
If any District Magistrate, Sub-divisional Magistrate, or Magistrate of the first class has reason to believe that any person is confined under circumstances that amount to an offence, he may issue a search-warrant. The person to whom the warrant is directed may search for the person so confined. If found, the person shall be immediately taken before a Magistrate, who shall make such order as appears proper.

Procedure: Search for Wrongfully Confined Person

Magistrate (District, Sub-divisional, or First Class) has reason to believe any person is confined under circumstances amounting to an offence

Magistrate may issue a search-warrant

Person to whom warrant is directed may search for the person so confined

Search shall be made in accordance with the warrant

If the person is found, he shall be immediately taken before a Magistrate

That Magistrate shall make such order as in the circumstances of the case seems proper

Illustration:
A District Magistrate receives reliable information that X is being held hostage in a basement against his will. The Magistrate issues a search-warrant to the police. Police break into the basement, find X, and immediately take him before the Magistrate. The Magistrate orders X's release and the arrest of the captors.

BNSS SECTION 101 - POWER TO COMPEL RESTORATION OF ABDUCTED FEMALES
Core Content:
Upon complaint made on oath of the abduction or unlawful detention of a woman or a female child for any unlawful purpose, a District Magistrate, Sub-divisional Magistrate, or Magistrate of first class may make an order for the immediate restoration of such woman to her liberty or such female child to her parent, guardian, or other person having lawful charge, and may compel compliance using necessary force.

Procedure: Restoration of Abducted Female

Complaint made on oath of:

(i) Abduction of a woman or female child for any unlawful purpose, OR

(ii) Unlawful detention of a woman or female child for any unlawful purpose

Magistrate (District, Sub-divisional, or First Class) may make order for immediate restoration:

(i) Of such woman to her liberty

(ii) Of such female child to her parent, guardian, or other person having lawful charge

Magistrate may compel compliance with such order

May use such force as may be necessary

Illustration:
A father complains on oath that his 14-year-old daughter has been abducted by X and is being held at X's house for unlawful purposes. The District Magistrate issues an order for immediate restoration of the daughter to her father. Police use force to enter X's house, recover the girl, and restore her to her father.

BNSS SECTION 102 - DIRECTION, ETC., OF SEARCH-WARRANTS
Core Content:
The provisions of sections 32, 72, 74, 76, 79, 80 and 81 shall, so far as may be, apply to all search-warrants issued under section 96, 97, 98, or 100.

Procedure: Application of Warrant Provisions to Search-Warrants

The following sections apply to search-warrants issued under sections 96, 97, 98, or 100:

Section 32 (Aid to person executing warrant)

Section 72 (Form of warrant of arrest and duration)

Section 74 (Warrants to whom directed)

Section 76 (Warrant directed to police officer)

Section 79 (Where warrant may be executed)

Section 80 (Warrant forwarded for execution outside jurisdiction)

Section 81 (Warrant directed to police officer for execution outside jurisdiction)

Apply "so far as may be" - as applicable to search-warrants

Illustration:
A search-warrant issued under section 97 can be executed at any place in India (section 79 applies). It can be forwarded to another jurisdiction for execution (section 80 applies). The provisions are applied as suitable for search-warrants rather than arrest warrants.

BNSS SECTION 103 - PERSONS IN CHARGE OF CLOSED PLACE TO ALLOW SEARCH
Core Content:
Whenever any place liable to search or inspection is closed, any person residing in or being in charge shall, on demand and production of warrant, allow free ingress and afford reasonable facilities. If ingress cannot be obtained, the officer may break open as per section 44(2). Two or more independent witnesses shall attend the search.

Procedure: Conducting Search of Closed Place

Any place liable to search or inspection under Chapter VII is closed

Person residing in or being in charge of such place shall:

(i) On demand of officer executing warrant

(ii) On production of warrant

(iii) Allow free ingress

(iv) Afford all reasonable facilities for search

If ingress cannot be obtained, officer may proceed as per section 44(2) (break open doors/windows)

If any person in or about such place is reasonably suspected of concealing an article for which search should be made:

Such person may be searched

If woman, search shall be made by another woman with strict regard to decency

Before making search, officer shall call upon two or more independent and respectable inhabitants of the locality (or any other locality if no such inhabitant available or willing) to attend and witness the search

Officer may issue order in writing to them to do so

Search shall be made in their presence

A list of all things seized and of places where found shall be prepared, signed by witnesses

No person witnessing search shall be required to attend Court as witness unless specially summoned

Occupant of place searched (or some person on his behalf) shall be permitted to attend during search

A copy of the list, signed by witnesses, shall be delivered to such occupant or person

When a person is searched, a list of things taken possession of shall be prepared and a copy delivered to such person

Any person who without reasonable cause refuses or neglects to attend and witness a search when called upon by order in writing shall be deemed to have committed an offence under BNS section 222

Illustration:
Police have a search-warrant for X's house. The house is locked. Police demand entry, show the warrant. X's wife refuses. Police break open the door (section 44(2)). Before searching, police call two neighbours to witness. A list of seized items (a stolen laptop, counterfeit currency) is prepared, signed by the neighbours, and a copy given to X's wife.

BNSS SECTION 104 - DISPOSAL OF THINGS FOUND IN SEARCH BEYOND JURISDICTION
Core Content:
When in execution of a search-warrant at a place beyond the local jurisdiction of the issuing Court, any thing for which search is made is found, such things with the list shall be immediately taken before the issuing Court, unless such place is nearer to the Magistrate having jurisdiction therein, in which case they shall be taken before such Magistrate who may order them to be taken to the issuing Court.

Procedure: Handling Seized Items from Another Jurisdiction

Search-warrant executed at a place beyond the local jurisdiction of the Court which issued the warrant

Things for which search was made are found

Such things together with the list prepared under section 103 shall be immediately taken before:

(i) The Court issuing the warrant, unless the place is nearer to the Magistrate having jurisdiction therein

(ii) If nearer to such Magistrate, then before that Magistrate

The Magistrate (if things taken before him) shall, unless good cause to the contrary, make an order authorising them to be taken to the issuing Court

Illustration:
Delhi Court issues search-warrant for a house in Gurugram (different jurisdiction). Police find counterfeit stamps. Since Gurugram is nearer to the Gurugram Magistrate than to Delhi Court, police take the stamps before the Gurugram Magistrate. The Gurugram Magistrate orders the stamps to be sent to Delhi Court.

BNSS SECTION 105 - RECORDING OF SEARCH AND SEIZURE THROUGH AUDIO-VIDEO ELECTRONIC MEANS
Core Content:
The process of conducting search of a place or taking possession of any property, article or thing under this Chapter or under section 185, including preparation of the list of all things seized and signing of such list by witnesses, shall be recorded through any audio-video electronic means (preferably mobile phone). The police officer shall without delay forward such recording to the District Magistrate, Sub-divisional Magistrate, or Judicial Magistrate of first class.

Procedure: Mandatory Audio-Video Recording of Search

Applies to search under Chapter VII (sections 94-110) OR under section 185 (search by police officer during investigation)

Process of conducting search of a place shall be recorded through audio-video electronic means (preferably mobile phone)

Process of taking possession of any property, article, or thing shall be recorded

Preparation of list of all things seized shall be recorded

Signing of such list by witnesses shall be recorded

Police officer shall without delay forward such recording to:

(i) District Magistrate, OR

(ii) Sub-divisional Magistrate, OR

(iii) Judicial Magistrate of first class

Illustration:
Police execute a search-warrant on X's house for stolen goods. A police officer records the entire search process on his mobile phone: breaking open the door, entering, finding stolen jewellery, preparing the seizure list, and witnesses signing the list. The video is immediately sent to the District Magistrate. This provides indisputable evidence of what was seized and where.

BNSS SECTION 106 - POWER OF POLICE OFFICER TO SEIZE CERTAIN PROPERTY
Core Content:
Any police officer may seize any property which may be alleged or suspected to have been stolen, or which may be found under circumstances creating suspicion of commission of any offence. He shall forthwith report the seizure to the Magistrate having jurisdiction. If property is subject to speedy decay and value is less than five hundred rupees, it may be sold by auction under Superintendent of Police orders.

Procedure: Police Seizure of Property

Any police officer may seize any property which:

(i) May be alleged or suspected to have been stolen, OR

(ii) May be found under circumstances which create suspicion of commission of any offence

If the police officer is subordinate to officer in charge of police station, he shall forthwith report the seizure to that officer

Every police officer shall forthwith report the seizure to the Magistrate having jurisdiction

If property seized cannot be conveniently transported to Court, OR there is difficulty securing proper accommodation for custody, OR continued retention in police custody is not necessary for investigation:

Police officer may give custody to any person on his executing a bond to produce the property before Court as and when required

Perishable property (value less than ₹500): If property is subject to speedy and natural decay, person entitled to possession is unknown or absent, and value is less than ₹500:

May forthwith be sold by auction under orders of Superintendent of Police

Sections 503 and 504 apply to net proceeds of such sale

Illustration:
Police officer finds a stolen bicycle in suspicious circumstances. He seizes it, reports to SHO and Magistrate. If the bicycle is old and worth ₹300, and no claimant appears, the Superintendent of Police may order it auctioned immediately before it rusts away.

BNSS SECTION 107 - ATTACHMENT, FORFEITURE OR RESTORATION OF PROPERTY
Core Content:
Where a police officer has reason to believe that any property is derived from criminal activity, he may with approval of Superintendent of Police/Commissioner of Police apply to the Court for attachment. The Court may issue notice to show cause within 14 days. If property is found to be proceeds of crime, the Court shall order the District Magistrate to rateably distribute it to persons affected by the crime.

Procedure: Attachment of Proceeds of Crime

Police officer making investigation has reason to believe any property is derived/obtained (directly or indirectly) as result of criminal activity or from commission of any offence

With approval of Superintendent of Police or Commissioner of Police, make application to Court or Magistrate exercising jurisdiction for attachment of such property

If Court/Magistrate has reasons to believe (before or after taking evidence) that all or any of such properties are proceeds of crime:

Court/Magistrate may issue notice upon such person calling upon him to show cause within 14 days why order of attachment shall not be made

If notice specifies property held by any other person on behalf of such person, copy shall also be served upon such other person

After considering explanation and material facts, and after giving reasonable opportunity of being heard, Court/Magistrate may pass order of attachment in respect of properties found to be proceeds of crime

If person does not appear within 14 days, Court may proceed ex parte

Interim order: If Court is of opinion that issuance of notice would defeat object of attachment, Court may by interim ex parte order direct attachment. Such order remains in force till order under sub-section (6) is passed

If Court finds attached/seized properties to be proceeds of crime, Court shall order District Magistrate to rateably distribute such proceeds of crime to persons affected by such crime

District Magistrate shall within 60 days distribute proceeds of crime (himself or through subordinate)

If no claimants or surplus after satisfying claimants, such proceeds of crime stand forfeited to Government

Illustration:
Investigation reveals that X acquired a luxury apartment worth ₹2 crore through drug money. Police, with SP approval, apply to Court for attachment. Court issues notice to X to show cause within 14 days. X fails to appear. Court attaches the apartment. After trial, Court finds it is proceeds of crime and orders District Magistrate to distribute the sale proceeds to victims of X's drug trafficking.

BNSS SECTION 108 - MAGISTRATE MAY DIRECT SEARCH IN HIS PRESENCE
Core Content:
Any Magistrate may direct a search to be made in his presence of any place for the search of which he is competent to issue a search-warrant.

Procedure: Search in Magistrate's Presence

Any Magistrate (Executive or Judicial)

Competent to issue search-warrant for a particular place

Magistrate may direct a search to be made in his presence

Magistrate personally oversees the search

Illustration:
A Magistrate is competent to issue a search-warrant for X's office. Instead of issuing a warrant to police, the Magistrate goes to X's office with the police and directs the search to be conducted in his presence. The Magistrate personally supervises to ensure proper procedure.

BNSS SECTION 109 - POWER TO IMPOUND DOCUMENT, ETC., PRODUCED
Core Content:
Any Court may, if it thinks fit, impound any document or thing produced before it under this Sanhita.

Procedure: Impounding Documents

Any Court (before which proceedings are pending)

Document or thing produced before it under BNSS

Court may, if it thinks fit, impound (take and retain custody of) such document or thing

Discretionary power of Court

Illustration:
During a trial, the prosecution produces an original contract document as evidence. The Court suspects it may be forged and wants to send it for forensic examination. The Court impounds the document, meaning it takes custody of it and keeps it in Court records.

BNSS SECTION 110 - RECIPROCAL ARRANGEMENTS REGARDING PROCESSES
Core Content:
Where a Court in BNSS territories desires that a summons, warrant, or search-warrant be served or executed at a place within India outside BNSS territories or in a contracting State (foreign country with reciprocal arrangements), it may send such process to the presiding officer of that Court or to such authority as Central Government specifies. Similarly, processes received from such Courts shall be executed as if received from another Court in BNSS territories.

Procedure: International and Inter-Jurisdictional Process

Sending processes from BNSS territories:

Court in BNSS territories desires summons/warrant be served/executed at a place:

(i) Within local jurisdiction of a Court in any State or area in India outside BNSS territories, OR

(ii) In any country or place outside India (contracting State with reciprocal arrangements)

May send such summons/warrant in duplicate by post or otherwise to the presiding officer of that Court or to such authority as Central Government notifies

For contracting State: send in such form directed to such Court/Judge/Magistrate as Central Government specifies

Receiving processes from outside BNSS territories:

Court in BNSS territories receives summons/warrant from:

(i) Court in any State or area in India outside BNSS territories, OR

(ii) Court/Judge/Magistrate in a contracting State

Shall cause same to be served/executed as if it were a summons/warrant received from another Court in BNSS territories within its local jurisdiction

If warrant of arrest executed: person arrested shall be dealt with as per sections 82 and 83

If search-warrant executed: things found shall be dealt with as per section 104

Illustration:
A Delhi Court wants to serve a summons on X who is in a remote area of a State where BNSS does not apply (e.g., tribal area). Delhi Court sends the summons to the local Court there, which serves it as if it were its own summons. India has a reciprocal arrangement with UAE. A Dubai Court sends a warrant for arrest of X who is in Mumbai. Mumbai police execute the warrant and follow sections 82-83 procedure.

BNSS SECTION 111 - DEFINITIONS
Core Content:
This section defines key terms for Chapter VIII including "contracting State" (any country outside India with reciprocal arrangements), "proceeds of crime" (any property derived directly or indirectly from criminal activity), "property" (assets of every description including corporeal/incorporeal, movable/immovable, tangible/intangible), and "tracing" (determining nature, source, disposition, movement, title or ownership of property).

Procedure: Understanding Key Definitions

"contracting State" - any country or place outside India in respect of which Central Government has made arrangements with that Government through treaty or otherwise

"identifying" - includes establishment of a proof that the property was derived from, or used in, the commission of an offence

"proceeds of crime" - any property derived or obtained directly or indirectly by any person as a result of criminal activity (including crime involving currency transfers) OR the value of any such property

"property" - property and assets of every description:

(i) Corporeal or incorporeal

(ii) Movable or immovable

(iii) Tangible or intangible

(iv) Deeds and instruments evidencing title to, or interest in, such property or assets derived or used in commission of an offence

(v) Includes property obtained through proceeds of crime

"tracing" - determining the nature, source, disposition, movement, title or ownership of property

Illustration:
India has a treaty with Singapore for mutual legal assistance. Singapore is a "contracting State." X earns ₹10 crore from drug trafficking and buys gold, stocks, and a house. All these assets are "proceeds of crime." The investigating agency wants to "trace" where X sent money through his bank accounts to determine the "property" that can be attached.

BNSS SECTION 112 - LETTER OF REQUEST TO COMPETENT AUTHORITY FOR INVESTIGATION IN A COUNTRY OR PLACE OUTSIDE INDIA
Core Content:
If during investigation of an offence, the investigating officer applies that evidence may be available in a country outside India, any Criminal Court may issue a letter of request to a Court or authority in that country to examine any person, record his statement, and require production of documents or things. The letter shall be transmitted as Central Government specifies. Every statement recorded or document/thing received shall be deemed evidence collected during investigation under BNSS.

Procedure: Sending Letter of Request (LOR)

During investigation into an offence, investigating officer or any officer superior to him makes application that evidence may be available in a country or place outside India

Any Criminal Court may issue a letter of request to:

A Court in that country, OR

An authority in that country competent to deal with such request

The LOR may request the foreign authority to:

(i) Examine orally any person supposed to be acquainted with facts and circumstances of the case

(ii) Record his statement made during such examination

(iii) Require such person or any other person to produce any document or thing in his possession pertaining to the case

(iv) Forward all evidence so taken or collected (or authenticated copies thereof) or the thing so collected to the Court issuing the LOR

The LOR shall be transmitted in such manner as the Central Government may specify

Every statement recorded or document/thing received under this section shall be deemed to be the evidence collected during the course of investigation under BNSS

Illustration:
During investigation of a money laundering case, the investigating officer learns that key documents are in a bank in Switzerland. The Court issues a Letter of Request to the Swiss competent authority asking them to obtain the bank records and examine the bank manager. The documents received from Switzerland become part of the investigation record in India.

BNSS SECTION 113 - LETTER OF REQUEST FROM A COUNTRY OR PLACE OUTSIDE INDIA TO A COURT OR AN AUTHORITY FOR INVESTIGATION IN INDIA
Core Content:
Upon receipt of a letter of request from a competent authority in a contracting State, the Central Government may forward it to the Chief Judicial Magistrate or a Judicial Magistrate to summon the person and record his statement or produce documents, or send it to a police officer for investigation as if the offence had been committed within India. All evidence taken shall be forwarded to the Central Government for transmission to the requesting authority.

Procedure: Executing Foreign Letter of Request in India

Central Government receives letter of request from a Court or authority in a contracting State competent to issue such letter

The request is for examination of any person or production of any document/thing in relation to an offence under investigation in that country

Central Government may (if thinks fit) choose either option:

Option (i): Forward the LOR to Chief Judicial Magistrate or a Judicial Magistrate (as he may appoint)

Such Magistrate shall summon the person before him

Record his statement OR cause the document/thing to be produced

Option (ii): Send the letter to any police officer for investigation

Such police officer shall investigate into the offence

In the same manner as if the offence had been committed within India

All evidence taken or collected (or authenticated copies thereof) or the thing so collected shall be forwarded by the Magistrate or police officer to the Central Government

Central Government transmits to the Court or authority issuing the letter of request, in such manner as it deems fit

Illustration:
A US Court sends a Letter of Request to India seeking examination of X, a witness in a US fraud case. The Central Government forwards the LOR to the Chief Judicial Magistrate of Delhi. The CJM summons X, records his statement, and forwards it to the Central Government, which transmits it to the US Court.

BNSS SECTION 114 - ASSISTANCE IN SECURING TRANSFER OF PERSONS
Core Content:
Where a Court in India desires arrest warrant execution in a contracting State, it shall send the warrant through Central Government to that State's Court/Judge/Magistrate. Similarly, if a person's attendance is required from a contracting State, the Court may issue summons/warrant. When a warrant is received from a contracting State, it shall be executed as if received from another Court in India. Prisoners transferred may be subject to conditions imposed by the Court or Central Government.

Procedure: Transfer of Persons Between Countries

Sending request from India:

Court in India desires that a warrant for arrest of any person to attend or produce a document issued by it shall be executed in any place in a contracting State

Court shall send such warrant in duplicate in such form to such Court/Judge/Magistrate through such authority as Central Government notifies

That foreign Court/Judge/Magistrate shall cause the same to be executed

Request for attendance from contracting State:

During investigation/inquiry into an offence, investigating officer or superior officer applies that attendance of a person in a contracting State is required

Court is satisfied that such attendance is so required

Court shall issue summons or warrant in duplicate against said person to such Court/Judge/Magistrate in such form as Central Government notifies

To cause the same to be served or executed

Receiving warrant from contracting State:

Court in India receives a warrant for arrest of any person requiring him to attend or attend and produce a document/thing, issued by a Court/Judge/Magistrate in a contracting State

Same shall be executed as if it is a warrant received from another Court in India for execution within its local limits

Conditions for prisoners:

If person transferred to contracting State is a prisoner in India: Court in India or Central Government may impose such conditions as deemed fit

If person transferred to India is a prisoner in contracting State: Court in India shall ensure conditions subject to which prisoner is transferred are complied with, and prisoner shall be kept in such custody subject to such conditions as Central Government directs in writing

Illustration:
Indian Court wants X, who is in UK prison, to testify in a trial in India. The Court issues a warrant and sends it to UK through Central Government. UK transfers X to India on condition he is returned after 30 days. Indian Court ensures X is kept in judicial custody and returned to UK after testimony.

BNSS SECTION 115 - ASSISTANCE IN RELATION TO ORDERS OF ATTACHMENT OR FORFEITURE OF PROPERTY
Core Content:
Where a Court in India has reasonable grounds to believe that any property is derived from commission of an offence, it may make an order of attachment or forfeiture. If such property is suspected to be in a contracting State, the Court may issue a letter of request for execution of such order. Similarly, when Central Government receives a letter of request from a contracting State for attachment/forfeiture of property in India, it may forward it to a Court for execution.

Procedure: Cross-Border Attachment/Forfeiture

India requesting contracting State:

Court in India has reasonable grounds to believe any property obtained by any person is derived (directly or indirectly) from commission of an offence

Court may make order of attachment or forfeiture of such property under sections 116 to 122

If such property is suspected to be in a contracting State, Court may issue letter of request to a Court or authority in the contracting State for execution of such order

Contracting State requesting India:

Central Government receives letter of request from a Court or authority in a contracting State requesting attachment or forfeiture of property in India

Property is derived (directly or indirectly) from commission of an offence committed in that contracting State

Central Government may forward such letter of request to such Court as it thinks fit

For execution in accordance with sections 116 to 122 or any other law for the time being in force

Illustration:
Indian Court finds evidence that X's property worth ₹5 crore in Dubai is proceeds of crime committed in India. Court issues attachment order and sends letter of request to Dubai Court. Dubai attaches the property. Similarly, UAE requests India to attach Y's property in Mumbai that is proceeds of crime in UAE. Central Government forwards request to Mumbai Court for attachment.

BNSS SECTION 116 - IDENTIFYING UNLAWFULLY ACQUIRED PROPERTY
Core Content:
The Court shall direct any police officer not below Sub-Inspector to take all steps necessary for tracing and identifying property under section 115. Such steps may include inquiry, investigation, or survey in respect of any person, place, property, assets, documents, books of account in any bank or public financial institutions, or any other relevant matters.

Procedure: Tracing Illegally Acquired Property

Court acts under section 115(1) (India requesting contracting State) OR on receipt of letter of request under section 115(3) (contracting State requesting India)

Court shall direct any police officer not below rank of Sub-Inspector of Police to take all steps necessary for tracing and identifying such property

Steps may include any inquiry, investigation, or survey in respect of:

(i) Any person

(ii) Any place

(iii) Any property

(iv) Any assets

(v) Any documents

(vi) Books of account in any bank or public financial institutions

(vii) Any other relevant matters

Such inquiry/investigation/survey shall be carried out in accordance with directions issued by the Court

Illustration:
Court orders tracing of X's illegally acquired properties. A Sub-Inspector is directed to investigate. The SI conducts inquiry into X's bank accounts, surveys his known properties, investigates his business documents, and traces money flow through multiple accounts to identify all assets purchased with crime proceeds.

BNSS SECTION 117 - SEIZURE OR ATTACHMENT OF PROPERTY
Core Content:
Where an officer conducting inquiry/investigation under section 116 has reason to believe that property is likely to be concealed, transferred, or dealt with resulting in disposal, he may make an order for seizure of such property, or if not practicable to seize, an order of attachment directing that it shall not be transferred or dealt with except with prior permission. Such order has no effect unless confirmed by the Court within thirty days.

Procedure: Interim Seizure/Attachment

Officer conducting inquiry/investigation under section 116 has reason to believe that any property in relation to which such inquiry/investigation is being conducted is likely to be:

(i) Concealed, OR

(ii) Transferred, OR

(iii) Dealt with in any manner which will result in disposal of such property

Officer may make an order for:

(i) Seizing such property, OR

(ii) If not practicable to seize, an order of attachment directing that such property shall not be transferred or otherwise dealt with except with prior permission of the officer making such order

A copy of such order shall be served on the person concerned

Critical limitation: Any order made under sub-section (1) shall have no effect unless the said order is confirmed by an order of the Court within a period of thirty days of its being made

Illustration:
Investigating officer finds that X is about to sell his luxury apartment purchased with crime proceeds. The officer seizes the property documents and serves order on X. However, this seizure is automatically invalid after 30 days unless the Court confirms it within that period. The officer must rush to Court for confirmation within 30 days.

BNSS SECTION 118 - MANAGEMENT OF PROPERTIES SEIZED OR FORFEITED UNDER THIS CHAPTER
Core Content:
The Court may appoint the District Magistrate of the area where property is situated (or any other officer nominated by the District Magistrate) as an Administrator to receive and manage the property. The Administrator shall manage the property as specified by the Central Government and take measures to dispose of property forfeited to the Central Government.

Procedure: Appointment of Administrator

Court may appoint:

(i) District Magistrate of the area where property is situated, OR

(ii) Any other officer nominated by the District Magistrate

Such person performs functions of an Administrator of such property

Administrator shall:

(i) Receive and manage the property in relation to which order has been made under section 117(1) or section 120

(ii) Manage in such manner and subject to such conditions as Central Government may specify

Administrator shall also take such measures as Central Government may direct to dispose of property which is forfeited to the Central Government

Illustration:
Court attaches X's five properties under section 117. The Court appoints the District Magistrate as Administrator. The DM takes possession of the properties, manages them (e.g., collects rent), and when properties are finally forfeited to Central Government, the DM disposes of them as per Central Government directions.

BNSS SECTION 119 - NOTICE OF FORFEITURE OF PROPERTY
Core Content:
If as a result of inquiry, investigation, or survey under section 116 the Court has reason to believe that properties are proceeds of crime, it may serve a notice upon the person affected calling upon him within thirty days to indicate the source of income, earnings or assets by which he acquired such property, and to show cause why such property should not be declared proceeds of crime and forfeited.

Procedure: Show Cause Notice for Forfeiture

As a result of inquiry, investigation, or survey under section 116, Court has reason to believe that all or any of such properties are proceeds of crime

Court may serve a notice upon the person affected (hereinafter referred to as the person affected)

Notice shall call upon the person affected within a period of thirty days specified in the notice to:

(i) Indicate the source of income, earnings or assets out of which or by means of which he has acquired such property

(ii) Indicate the evidence on which he relies

(iii) Provide other relevant information and particulars

(iv) Show cause why all or any of such properties should not be declared to be proceeds of crime and forfeited to the Central Government

Where notice to any person specifies any property as being held on behalf of such person by any other person, a copy of the notice shall also be served upon such other person

Illustration:
Court investigation reveals X owns properties worth ₹10 crore but his known income is only ₹50 lakh per year. Court issues notice to X: "Show cause within 30 days why your properties should not be forfeited. Explain the source of funds used to purchase these properties." X must provide bank statements, gift deeds, or other evidence showing legitimate source.

BNSS SECTION 120 - FORFEITURE OF PROPERTY IN CERTAIN CASES
Core Content:
The Court may, after considering explanation to show-cause notice under section 119 and material available, and after giving reasonable opportunity of being heard, record a finding whether any property is proceeds of crime. If Court is satisfied that some properties are proceeds of crime but cannot specifically identify them, it may specify properties to the best of its judgment. Such property shall stand forfeited to Central Government free from all encumbrances.

Procedure: Final Forfeiture Order

Court considers:

(i) Explanation, if any, to show-cause notice issued under section 119

(ii) Material available before it

Court gives reasonable opportunity of being heard to:

(i) The person affected, AND

(ii) If person affected holds property through any other person, to such other person also

Court by order records a finding whether all or any of the properties in question are proceeds of crime

Ex parte proceeding: If person affected (and other person if applicable) does not appear or represent case within 30 days specified in show-cause notice, Court may proceed ex parte on basis of evidence available

Unidentifiable proceeds: If Court is satisfied that some properties are proceeds of crime but it is not possible to identify specifically such properties, Court may specify the properties which, to the best of its judgment, are proceeds of crime and record finding accordingly

Where Court records finding that any property is proceeds of crime, such property shall stand forfeited to the Central Government free from all encumbrances

If shares in a company stand forfeited, the company shall immediately register the Central Government as transferee of such shares (notwithstanding anything in Companies Act, 2013 or Articles of Association)

Illustration:
Court finds X's property is proceeds of drug trafficking. The property has a mortgage from a bank. Upon forfeiture order, the property goes to Central Government free from the mortgage. The bank's encumbrance is wiped out. If X holds shares in a company, the company must register Central Government as the new shareholder.

BNSS SECTION 121 - FINE IN LIEU OF FORFEITURE
Core Content:
Where the Court declares property forfeited under section 120 but the source of only a part of such property has not been proved, the Court shall give an option to the person affected to pay, in lieu of forfeiture, a fine equal to the market value of such part. If the person pays the fine within the allowed time, the Court shall revoke the declaration of forfeiture and release the property.

Procedure: Fine Instead of Forfeiture

Court makes declaration that any property stands forfeited to Central Government under section 120

It is a case where the source of only a part of such property has not been proved to the satisfaction of the Court

Court shall make an order giving an option to the person affected to pay, in lieu of forfeiture, a fine equal to the market value of such part

Before making order imposing fine, person affected shall be given reasonable opportunity of being heard

Where person affected pays the fine due within such time as may be allowed, Court may by order revoke the declaration of forfeiture under section 120

Thereupon such property shall stand released

Illustration:
X owns 3 properties worth ₹1 crore each. Court finds that source of funds for 2 properties (₹2 crore) is unexplained, but source for 1 property (₹1 crore) is legitimate. Court offers X an option: pay fine of ₹1 crore (market value of the 2 unexplained properties) instead of forfeiting all 3 properties. If X pays within allowed time, all 3 properties are released to X.

BNSS SECTION 122 - CERTAIN TRANSFERS TO BE NULL AND VOID
Core Content:
Where after the making of an order under section 117(1) (seizure/attachment) or the issue of a notice under section 119 (forfeiture notice), any property referred to in the order or notice is transferred by any mode whatsoever, such transfers shall be ignored for the purposes of proceedings under this Chapter. If such property is subsequently forfeited to the Central Government under section 120, the transfer of such property shall be deemed to be null and void.

Procedure: Avoiding Fraudulent Transfers

An order has been made under section 117(1) (seizure/attachment order) OR a notice has been issued under section 119 (forfeiture show-cause notice)

After such order or notice, any property referred to in the said order or notice is transferred by any mode whatsoever

Effect: Such transfers shall, for the purposes of the proceedings under this Chapter, be ignored

If such property is subsequently forfeited to the Central Government under section 120, then:

The transfer of such property shall be deemed to be null and void

Illustration:
Court issues attachment order on X's house under section 117(1). Before the attachment is enforced, X sells the house to Y who pays full value. When the property is later forfeited, the sale to Y is treated as null and void. Central Government takes the house from Y, and Y must sue X to recover the money paid.

BNSS SECTION 123 - PROCEDURE IN RESPECT OF LETTER OF REQUEST
Core Content:
Every letter of request, summons or warrant received by the Central Government from, and every letter of request, summons or warrant to be transmitted to, a contracting State under this Chapter shall be transmitted to a contracting State or sent to the concerned Court in India in such form and in such manner as the Central Government may, by notification, specify.

Procedure: Transmission of Legal Processes

Applies to:

(i) Letter of request

(ii) Summons

(iii) Warrant

Two scenarios:

Received by Central Government from a contracting State

To be transmitted to a contracting State

Such processes shall be:

(i) Transmitted to a contracting State, OR

(ii) Sent to the concerned Court in India

In such form and in such manner as the Central Government may, by notification, specify

Illustration:
Central Government issues a notification prescribing Form LOR-1 for sending Letters of Request to contracting States, and requiring transmission through the Ministry of External Affairs. All courts must use this prescribed form and channel when seeking assistance from or responding to contracting States.

BNSS SECTION 124 - APPLICATION OF THIS CHAPTER
Core Content:
The Central Government may, by notification in the Official Gazette, direct that the application of this Chapter in relation to a contracting State with which reciprocal arrangements have been made, shall be subject to such conditions, exceptions or qualifications as are specified in the said notification.

Procedure: Conditional Application to Contracting States

Central Government has made reciprocal arrangements with a contracting State

Central Government may issue a notification in the Official Gazette

The notification may direct that the application of Chapter VIII in relation to that contracting State shall be subject to:

(i) Such conditions

(ii) Such exceptions

(iii) Such qualifications

as are specified in the notification

Illustration:
India has reciprocal arrangements with Country X. Central Government issues a notification stating that Chapter VIII shall apply to Country X with the following conditions: (1) Letters of Request must be in English only, (2) No request for forfeiture of property valued under ₹10 lakh shall be entertained, (3) Requests must be routed through the Ministry of Home Affairs. Courts must follow these conditions when dealing with Country X.

BNSS SECTION 125 - SECURITY FOR KEEPING PEACE ON CONVICTION
Core Content:
When a Court of Session or Magistrate of first class convicts a person of certain offences (including offences under Chapter XI of BNS, assault, criminal force, mischief, criminal intimidation, or any offence causing breach of peace), and is of opinion that it is necessary to take security for keeping the peace, the Court may at the time of passing sentence order him to execute a bond or bail bond for keeping the peace for a period not exceeding three years.

Procedure: Security on Conviction

Court of Session or Magistrate of first class convicts a person of:

(a) Any offence punishable under Chapter XI of BNS (other than section 193(1), 196, or 197)

(b) Any offence which consists of or includes assault or using criminal force or committing mischief

(c) Any offence of criminal intimidation

(d) Any other offence which caused, or was intended or known to be likely to cause, a breach of the peace

OR abetment of any such offence

Court is of opinion that it is necessary to take security from such person for keeping the peace

Court may, at the time of passing sentence, order him to execute a bond or bail bond for keeping the peace

Period: not exceeding three years

If conviction is set aside on appeal or otherwise, the bond or bail bond shall become void

An Appellate Court or Court exercising revision powers may also make such an order

Illustration:
X is convicted of assault (criminal force) which caused a breach of peace. The Magistrate believes X may repeat such behaviour after release. At sentencing, the Magistrate orders X to execute a bond of ₹10,000 to keep the peace for 2 years. If X's conviction is later overturned on appeal, the bond becomes void automatically.

BNSS SECTION 126 - SECURITY FOR KEEPING PEACE IN OTHER CASES
Core Content:
When an Executive Magistrate receives information that any person is likely to commit a breach of the peace or disturb public tranquillity or do any wrongful act that may probably occasion a breach of peace, and is of opinion that there is sufficient ground for proceeding, he may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond or bail bond for keeping the peace for a period not exceeding one year.

Procedure: Preventive Security for Peace

Executive Magistrate receives information that any person:

(i) Is likely to commit a breach of the peace, OR

(ii) Is likely to disturb the public tranquillity, OR

(iii) Is likely to do any wrongful act that may probably occasion a breach of the peace or disturb public tranquillity

Magistrate is of opinion that there is sufficient ground for proceeding

Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond or bail bond for keeping the peace

Period: not exceeding one year

Proceedings may be taken before any Executive Magistrate when:

(i) The place where breach of peace or disturbance is apprehended is within his local jurisdiction, OR

(ii) There is within such jurisdiction a person who is likely to commit breach of peace or disturb tranquillity or do wrongful act beyond such jurisdiction

Illustration:
Executive Magistrate receives credible information that X, a known troublemaker, is planning to disrupt a religious festival next week which will likely cause a riot. The Magistrate issues a notice to X to show cause why he should not execute a bond for ₹20,000 to keep the peace for 6 months.

BNSS SECTION 127 - SECURITY FOR GOOD BEHAVIOUR FROM PERSONS DISSEMINATING CERTAIN MATTERS
Core Content:
When an Executive Magistrate receives information that any person within his jurisdiction intentionally disseminates matter punishable under BNS sections 152, 196, 197, 299 (against national integration, enmity between groups, religious feelings) or criminal intimidation/defamation against a Judge, or obscene matter under section 294, the Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond or bail bond for good behaviour for a period not exceeding one year.

Procedure: Security for Disseminating Objectionable Matter

Executive Magistrate receives information that within his local jurisdiction any person (within or without such jurisdiction):

(i) Either orally or in writing or in any other manner, intentionally disseminates or attempts to disseminate or abets dissemination of:

(a) Matter punishable under BNS section 152 (endangering sovereignty/unity/integrity), 196 (promoting enmity between groups), 197 (imputations prejudicial to national integration), or 299 (outraging religious feelings), OR

(b) Any matter concerning a Judge acting or purporting to act in discharge of official duties which amounts to criminal intimidation or defamation under BNS

(ii) Makes, produces, publishes or keeps for sale, imports, exports, conveys, sells, lets to hire, distributes, publicly exhibits or in any other manner puts into circulation any obscene matter under BNS section 294

Magistrate is of opinion that there is sufficient ground for proceeding

Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond or bail bond for his good behaviour

Period: not exceeding one year

Protection for press: No proceedings shall be taken against editor, proprietor, printer, or publisher of a registered publication under Press and Registration of Books Act, 1867 with reference to any matter contained in such publication except by order or under authority of State Government or officer empowered by State Government

Illustration:
X is repeatedly posting inflammatory content online promoting enmity between religious groups (BNS section 196). Executive Magistrate issues notice to X to show cause why he should not execute a bond for good behaviour for 6 months. X runs a registered newspaper - the article appeared in his newspaper, so proceedings require State Government authorization.

BNSS SECTION 128 - SECURITY FOR GOOD BEHAVIOUR FROM SUSPECTED PERSONS
Core Content:
When an Executive Magistrate receives information that there is within his local jurisdiction a person taking precautions to conceal his presence and that there is reason to believe that he is doing so with a view to committing a cognizable offence, the Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond or bail bond for his good behaviour for a period not exceeding one year.

Procedure: Security for Suspicious Persons

Executive Magistrate receives information that there is within his local jurisdiction a person:

(i) Taking precautions to conceal his presence, AND

(ii) There is reason to believe that he is doing so with a view to committing a cognizable offence

Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond or bail bond for his good behaviour

Period: not exceeding one year

Illustration:
Police inform Executive Magistrate that an unknown person has been seen lurking around the bank at night, wearing a mask and hiding behind bushes, taking precautions to conceal his presence. The Magistrate issues notice to the person (once identified) to show cause why he should not give a bond for good behaviour for 6 months.

BNSS SECTION 129 - SECURITY FOR GOOD BEHAVIOUR FROM HABITUAL OFFENDERS
Core Content:
When an Executive Magistrate receives information that there is within his local jurisdiction a person who is by habit a robber, house-breaker, thief, forger, receiver of stolen property, harbourer of thieves, or habitually commits kidnapping, abduction, extortion, cheating, mischief, or offences under specified Acts (Drugs and Cosmetics Act, Foreigners Act, Employees' Provident Fund Act, Essential Commodities Act, Protection of Civil Rights Act, Customs Act, Food Safety Act, etc.), or is so desperate and dangerous as to render his being at large without security hazardous to the community, the Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bail bond for his good behaviour for a period not exceeding three years.

Procedure: Security for Habitual Offenders

Executive Magistrate receives information that within his local jurisdiction a person:

(a) Is by habit a robber, house-breaker, thief, or forger

(b) Is by habit a receiver of stolen property knowing it to have been stolen

(c) Habitually protects or harbours thieves, or aids in concealment or disposal of stolen property

(d) Habitually commits, attempts, or abets kidnapping, abduction, extortion, cheating, mischief, or offences under BNS Chapter X (offences against property) or sections 178-181 (counterfeiting)

(e) Habitually commits, attempts, or abets offences involving breach of peace

(f) Habitually commits, attempts, or abets any offence under: Drugs and Cosmetics Act 1940, Foreigners Act 1946, Employees' Provident Fund Act 1952, Essential Commodities Act 1955, Protection of Civil Rights Act 1955, Customs Act 1962, Food Safety and Standards Act 2006, or any other law preventing hoarding/profiteering/adultration/corruption

(g) Is so desperate and dangerous as to render his being at large without security hazardous to the community

Magistrate may require such person to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bail bond for his good behaviour

Period: not exceeding three years

Illustration:
X has three prior convictions for theft and is known to be a habitual thief. He was just released from jail. Police inform the Executive Magistrate. The Magistrate issues notice to X to show cause why he should not execute a bail bond of ₹50,000 with two sureties for good behaviour for 2 years.

BNSS SECTION 130 - ORDER TO BE MADE
Core Content:
When a Magistrate acting under sections 126, 127, 128, or 129 deems it necessary to require any person to show cause, he shall make an order in writing setting forth the substance of the information received, the amount of the bond to be executed, the term for which it is to be in force, and the number of sureties, after considering the sufficiency and fitness of sureties.

Procedure: Written Show Cause Order

Magistrate acting under section 126, 127, 128, or 129 deems it necessary to require any person to show cause

Magistrate shall make an order in writing setting forth:

(i) Substance of the information received

(ii) Amount of the bond to be executed

(iii) Term for which it is to be in force

(iv) Number of sureties

Magistrate shall consider the sufficiency and fitness of sureties

Illustration:
Magistrate issues written order to X: "Information received that you are likely to commit breach of peace. You are required to show cause why you should not execute a bond of ₹10,000 with one surety of ₹10,000 for keeping peace for 6 months."

BNSS SECTION 131 - PROCEDURE IN RESPECT OF PERSON PRESENT IN COURT
Core Content:
If the person in respect of whom such order is made is present in Court, it shall be read over to him, or if he so desires, the substance thereof shall be explained to him.

Procedure: Reading Order to Person Present

Order under section 130 has been made

The person in respect of whom order is made is present in Court

The order shall be read over to him

If he so desires, the substance thereof shall be explained to him

Illustration:
X is present in Court when the Magistrate issues the show cause order under section 130. The Magistrate reads the order aloud to X. X says he does not fully understand legal terms. The Magistrate explains the substance in simple language.

BNSS SECTION 132 - SUMMONS OR WARRANT IN CASE OF PERSON NOT SO PRESENT
Core Content:
If such person is not present in Court, the Magistrate shall issue a summons requiring him to appear, or if he is in custody, a warrant directing the officer to bring him before the Court. If there is reason to fear commission of breach of peace that cannot be prevented otherwise than by immediate arrest, the Magistrate may at any time issue a warrant for his arrest.

Procedure: Compelling Appearance

Person is not present in Court

Magistrate shall issue a summons requiring him to appear

If such person is in custody: issue a warrant directing the officer in whose custody he is to bring him before the Court

Emergency warrant: If it appears to Magistrate (upon police report or other information, substance recorded) that:

(i) There is reason to fear commission of a breach of the peace, AND

(ii) Such breach of peace cannot be prevented otherwise than by immediate arrest of such person

Then Magistrate may at any time issue a warrant for his arrest

Illustration:
X is not present in Court. Magistrate issues summons for X to appear. However, police report that X is gathering a mob right now to attack a neighbourhood. Magistrate records this information and immediately issues an arrest warrant to prevent imminent breach of peace.

BNSS SECTION 133 - COPY OF ORDER TO ACCOMPANY SUMMONS OR WARRANT
Core Content:
Every summons or warrant issued under section 132 shall be accompanied by a copy of the order made under section 130, and such copy shall be delivered by the officer serving or executing such summons or warrant to the person served with, or arrested under, the same.

Procedure: Providing Copy of Order

Summons or warrant issued under section 132

Shall be accompanied by a copy of the order made under section 130

The officer serving or executing the summons or warrant shall deliver the copy to:

(i) The person served with the summons, OR

(ii) The person arrested under the warrant

Illustration:
Police officer serves summons on X. Along with the summons, the officer hands X a copy of the Magistrate's written order under section 130 showing the substance of information, bond amount, term, and number of sureties.

BNSS SECTION 134 - POWER TO DISPENSE WITH PERSONAL ATTENDANCE
Core Content:
The Magistrate may, if he sees sufficient cause, dispense with the personal attendance of any person called upon to show cause why he should not be ordered to execute a bond for keeping the peace or for good behaviour and may permit him to appear by an advocate.

Procedure: Appearance by Advocate

Person is called upon to show cause under sections 125-129

Magistrate sees sufficient cause to dispense with personal attendance

Magistrate may permit the person to appear by an advocate

The advocate represents the person instead of the person attending personally

Illustration:
X is bedridden due to illness and cannot attend Court. Magistrate accepts medical certificate as sufficient cause and allows X's lawyer to appear on his behalf for the show cause proceedings.

BNSS SECTION 135 - INQUIRY AS TO TRUTH OF INFORMATION
Core Content:
The Magistrate shall proceed to inquire into the truth of the information upon which action has been taken. The inquiry shall be conducted in the manner prescribed for trial and recording evidence in summons-cases. The inquiry shall be completed within six months from commencement, otherwise proceedings shall terminate. If person is kept in detention pending inquiry, proceedings terminate on expiry of six months of such detention.

Procedure: Inquiry into Information

After order under section 130 has been read/explained under section 131 OR person appears/is brought before Magistrate under section 132

Magistrate shall proceed to inquire into the truth of the information upon which action has been taken

Magistrate shall take such further evidence as may appear necessary

Inquiry shall be made, as nearly as practicable, in the manner prescribed for conducting trial and recording evidence in summons-cases

Interim bond: After commencement but before completion of inquiry, if Magistrate considers immediate measures necessary for prevention of breach of peace or commission of offence, he may for reasons recorded direct the person to execute a bond for keeping peace or maintaining good behaviour until conclusion of inquiry, and may detain him in custody until bond is executed
